<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Milk</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">White liquid produced by the mammary glands of mammals</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the fluid produced by the mammary glands of mammals. For the milk-like beverages derived from plants, see <a href="/wiki/Plant_milk" title="Plant milk">Plant milk</a>. For other uses of the word, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Milk_(disambiguation)" title="Milk (disambiguation)">Milk (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glass_of_Milk_(33657535532).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1963" data-file-width="1958" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Glass_of_Milk_%2833657535532%29.jpg/220px-Glass_of_Milk_%2833657535532%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Glass_of_Milk_%2833657535532%29.jpg/330px-Glass_of_Milk_%2833657535532%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Glass_of_Milk_%2833657535532%29.jpg/440px-Glass_of_Milk_%2833657535532%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glass_of_Milk_(33657535532).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A glass of cow milk</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melkkarussell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Melkkarussell.jpg/220px-Melkkarussell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Melkkarussell.jpg/330px-Melkkarussell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Melkkarussell.jpg/440px-Melkkarussell.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melkkarussell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cows in a <a href="/wiki/Rotolactor" title="Rotolactor">rotary milking parlor</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Milk</b> is a white liquid <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> produced by the <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary glands</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. It is the primary source of <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a> for young mammals (including <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfed</a> human infants) before they are able to <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digest</a> solid food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Immune factors and immune-modulating components in milk contribute to <a href="/wiki/Milk_immunity" title="Milk immunity">milk immunity</a>. Early-<a href="/wiki/Lactation" title="Lactation">lactation</a> milk, which is called <a href="/wiki/Colostrum" title="Colostrum">colostrum</a>, contains <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a> that strengthen the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>, and thus reduces the risk of many diseases. Milk contains many nutrients, including <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>As an agricultural product, <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a> milk is <a href="/wiki/Milking" title="Milking">collected from farm animals</a>. In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">dairy farms</a> produced around 730 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (800 million short tons) of milk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> from 260 million dairy cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is the world's largest producer of milk and the leading exporter of <a href="/wiki/Skimmed_milk" title="Skimmed milk">skimmed milk</a> powder, but it exports few other milk products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Because there is an ever-increasing demand for dairy products within India, it could eventually become a net importer of dairy products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> New Zealand, Germany and the Netherlands are the largest exporters of milk products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">US CDC</a> recommends that children over the age of 12 months should have two servings of dairy milk products a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than six billion people worldwide consume milk and milk products, and between 750 and 900 million people live in dairy-farming households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_terminology">Etymology and terminology</span></h2>
<p>The term <i>milk</i> comes from "Old English <i>meoluc</i> (West Saxon), <i>milc</i> (Anglian), from Proto-Germanic *<i>meluks</i> "milk" (source also of Old Norse <i>mjolk</i>, Old Frisian <i>melok</i>, Old Saxon <i>miluk</i>, Dutch <i>melk</i>, Old High German <i>miluh</i>, German <i>Milch</i>, Gothic <i>miluks</i>)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline.com_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline.com-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In food use, from 1961, the term <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/milk#Noun" title="wiktionary:milk"><i>milk</i></a> has been defined under <a href="/wiki/Codex_Alimentarius" title="Codex Alimentarius">Codex Alimentarius</a> standards as: "the normal mammary secretion of milking animals obtained from one or more milkings without either addition to it or extraction from it, intended for consumption as liquid milk or for further processing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The term <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dairy#Noun" title="wiktionary:dairy"><i>dairy</i></a> relates to animal milk and animal milk production.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_consumption">Types of consumption</span></h2>
<p>There are two distinct categories of milk consumption: all infant mammals drink milk directly from their mothers’ bodies, and it is their primary source of nutrition; and humans obtain milk from other mammals for consumption by humans of all ages, as one component of a varied diet.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition_for_infant_mammals">Nutrition for infant mammals</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">Breastfeeding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lactation" title="Lactation">Lactation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="998" data-file-width="738" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg/170px-Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg/255px-Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg/340px-Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woman_breastfeeding_an_infant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Breastfeeding to provide a mother's milk</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg/220px-Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg/330px-Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg/440px-Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goat_kid_feeding_on_mothers_milk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A goat kid feeding on its mother's milk</div></div></div>
<p>In almost all mammals, milk is fed to <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infants</a> through <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a>, either directly or by <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding#Expressed_milk" title="Breastfeeding">expressing</a> the milk to be stored and consumed later. The early milk from mammals is called <a href="/wiki/Colostrum" title="Colostrum">colostrum</a>. Colostrum contains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> that provide protection to the newborn baby as well as nutrients and growth factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The makeup of the colostrum and the period of secretion varies from species to species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>For humans, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> recommends exclusive breastfeeding for six months and breastfeeding in addition to other food for up to two years of age or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In some cultures it is common to breastfeed children for three to five years, and the period may be longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fresh goats' milk is sometimes substituted for <a href="/wiki/Breast_milk" title="Breast milk">breast milk</a>, which introduces the risk of the child developing <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> imbalances, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_acidosis" title="Metabolic acidosis">metabolic acidosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megaloblastic_anemia" title="Megaloblastic anemia">megaloblastic anemia</a>, and a host of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergic_reactions" title="Allergic reactions">allergic reactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_product_for_humans">Food product for humans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="2190" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/220px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/330px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/440px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Holstein_Friesian_cattle" title="Holstein Friesian cattle">Holstein Friesian cow</a> is the dominant breed in industrialized dairy farms today</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="807" data-file-width="1038" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png/220px-A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png/330px-A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png/440px-A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_bowl_of_milk_for_the_shaman_rite._Buryatia._Russia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bowl of milk for the shaman rite; <a href="/wiki/Buryatia" title="Buryatia">Buryatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a></div></div></div>
<p>In many cultures, especially in the West, humans continue to consume milk beyond infancy, using the milk of other mammals (especially cattle, goats and sheep) as a food product. Initially, the ability to digest milk was limited to children as adults did not produce <a href="/wiki/Lactase" title="Lactase">lactase</a>, an enzyme necessary for digesting the lactose in milk. People therefore converted milk to <a href="/wiki/Curd" title="Curd">curd</a>, cheese, and other products to reduce the levels of lactose. Thousands of years ago, a chance mutation spread in human populations in northwestern Europe that enabled the <a href="/wiki/Lactase_persistence" title="Lactase persistence">production of lactase in adulthood</a>. This mutation allowed milk to be used as a new source of nutrition which could sustain populations when other food sources failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_Milk_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_Milk-18">[18]</a></sup> Milk is processed into a variety of products such as <a href="/wiki/Cream" title="Cream">cream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butter" title="Butter">butter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kefir" title="Kefir">kefir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice cream</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a>. Modern industrial processes use milk to produce <a href="/wiki/Casein" title="Casein">casein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whey_protein" title="Whey protein">whey protein</a>, lactose, <a href="/wiki/Condensed_milk" title="Condensed milk">condensed milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Powdered_milk" title="Powdered milk">powdered milk</a>, and many other food-additives and industrial products.
</p><p>Whole milk, butter, and cream have high levels of <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The sugar lactose is found only in milk, and possibly in <a href="/wiki/Forsythia" title="Forsythia">forsythia</a> flowers and a few tropical shrubs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Lactase, the enzyme needed to digest lactose, reaches its highest levels in the human small intestine immediately after birth, and then begins a slow decline unless milk is consumed regularly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup> Those groups who continue to tolerate milk have often exercised great creativity in using the milk of <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a>, not only cattle, but also sheep, goats, <a href="/wiki/Yak" title="Yak">yaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>, horses, <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camel_milk" title="Camel milk">camels</a>. India is the largest producer and consumer of cattle- and buffalo milk in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501,_Berlin,_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="570" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501%2C_Berlin%2C_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501%2C_Berlin%2C_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501%2C_Berlin%2C_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501%2C_Berlin%2C_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501%2C_Berlin%2C_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501%2C_Berlin%2C_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-2004-0708-501,_Berlin,_Milch_von_Bolle_in_der_Pause.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drinking milk in Germany in 1932</div></div></div>
<p>Humans first learned to consume the milk of other mammals regularly following the domestication of animals during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> or the development of agriculture. This development occurred independently in several global locations from as early as 9000–7000<span class="nowrap"> </span>BC in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> to 3500–3000<span class="nowrap"> </span>BC in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> People first domesticated the most important dairy animals – cattle, sheep and goats – in Southwest Asia, although domestic cattle had been independently derived from wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> populations several times since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Initially animals were kept for meat, and archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sherratt" title="Andrew Sherratt">Andrew Sherratt</a> has suggested that dairying, along with the exploitation of domestic animals for hair and labor, began much later in a separate <a href="/wiki/Secondary_products_revolution" title="Secondary products revolution">secondary products revolution</a> in the fourth millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Sherratt's model is not supported by recent findings, based on the analysis of <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> residue in prehistoric pottery, that shows that dairying was practiced in the early phases of agriculture in Southwest Asia, by at least the seventh millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>From Southwest Asia domestic dairy animals spread to Europe (beginning around 7000 BC but did not reach Britain and Scandinavia until after 4000 BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and South Asia (7000–5500 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The first farmers in central Europe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and Britain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> milked their animals. <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">Pastoral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nomadic_pastoralism" title="Nomadic pastoralism">pastoral nomadic</a> economies, which rely predominantly or exclusively on domestic animals and their products rather than crop farming, were developed as European farmers moved into the <a href="/wiki/Pontic%E2%80%93Caspian_steppe" title="Pontic–Caspian steppe">Pontic–Caspian steppe</a> in the fourth millennium BC, and subsequently spread across much of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian_steppe" title="Eurasian steppe">Eurasian steppe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Sheep and goats were introduced to Africa from Southwest Asia, but African cattle may have been independently domesticated around 7000–6000<span class="nowrap"> </span>BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Camels, domesticated in central Arabia in the fourth millennium BC, have also been used as dairy animals in North Africa and the Arabian Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The earliest Egyptian records of burn treatments describe burn dressings using milk from mothers of male babies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pećanac-_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pećanac--38">[38]</a></sup> In the rest of the world (i.e., East and Southeast Asia, the Americas and Australia) milk and dairy products were historically not a large part of the diet, either because they remained populated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a> who did not keep animals or the local agricultural economies did not include domesticated dairy species. Milk consumption became common in these regions comparatively recently, as a consequence of European <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> and political domination over much of the world in the last 500 years.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, milk was called the "virtuous white liquor" because alcoholic beverages were safer to consume than the water generally available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Rosier" title="James Rosier">James Rosier's</a> record of the 1605 voyage made by <a href="/wiki/George_Weymouth" title="George Weymouth">George Weymouth</a> to New England did report the <a href="/wiki/Wabanaki_Confederacy" title="Wabanaki Confederacy">Wabanaki</a> people Weymouth captured in Maine did report milking "Rain-Deere and Fallo-Deere." But Journalist <a href="/wiki/Avery_Yale_Kamila" title="Avery Yale Kamila">Avery Yale Kamila</a> and food historians said Rosier "misinterpreted the evidence." Historians report the Wabanaki did not domesticate deer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The tribes of the northern woodlands have historically been making <a href="/wiki/Plant_milk" title="Plant milk">nut milk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Cows were imported to New England in 1624.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrialization">Industrialization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1362" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg/220px-SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg/330px-SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg/440px-SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SR_4409_6_Wheeled_Milk_Wagon_Didcot_Railway_Centre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Preserved <a href="/wiki/Express_Dairies" title="Express Dairies">Express Dairies</a> three-axle milk tank wagon at the <a href="/wiki/Didcot_Railway_Centre" title="Didcot Railway Centre">Didcot Railway Centre</a>, based on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Railway_(Great_Britain)" title="Southern Railway (Great Britain)">SR</a> chassis</div></div></div>
<p>The growth in urban population, coupled with the expansion of the railway network in the mid-19th century, brought about a revolution in milk production and supply. Individual railway firms began transporting milk from rural areas to London from the 1840s and 1850s. Possibly the first such instance was in 1846, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27s_Hospital" title="St Thomas's Hospital">St Thomas's Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a> contracted with milk suppliers outside London to ship milk by rail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins-44">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Railway" title="Great Western Railway">Great Western Railway</a> was an early and enthusiastic adopter, and began to transport milk into London from <a href="/wiki/Maidenhead" title="Maidenhead">Maidenhead</a> in 1860, despite much criticism. By 1900, the company was transporting over 25 million imperial gallons (110 million litres; 30 million US gallons) annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The milk trade grew slowly through the 1860s, but went through a period of extensive, structural change in the 1870s and 1880s.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg/220px-Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg/330px-Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg/440px-Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aesthetic_Milk_Vehicle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Milk transportation in <a href="/wiki/Salem,_Tamil_Nadu" title="Salem, Tamil Nadu">Salem, Tamil Nadu</a></div></div></div>
<p>Urban demand began to grow, as consumer purchasing power increased and milk became regarded as a required daily commodity. Over the last three decades of the 19th century, demand for milk in most parts of the country doubled or, in some cases, tripled. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_Health_Acts" title="Public Health Acts">Legislation in 1875</a> made the adulteration of milk illegal<span class="nowrap"> </span>– This combined with a marketing campaign to change the image of milk. The proportion of rural imports by rail as a percentage of total milk consumption in London grew from under 5% in the 1860s to over 96% by the early 20th century. By that point, the supply system for milk was the most highly organized and integrated of any food product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins-44">[44]</a></sup> Milk was analyzed for infection with <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. In 1907 180 samples were tested in Birmingham and 13.3% were found to be infected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The first glass bottle packaging for milk was used in the 1870s. The first company to do so may have been the New York Dairy Company in 1877. The <a href="/wiki/Express_Dairies" title="Express Dairies">Express Dairy Company</a> in England began glass bottle production in 1880. In 1884, Hervey Thatcher, an American inventor from New York, invented a glass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_bottle" title="Milk bottle">milk bottle</a>, called "Thatcher's Common Sense Milk Jar," which was sealed with a waxed paper disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milk_history_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-milk_history-47">[47]</a></sup> Later, in 1932, <a href="/wiki/Plastic-coated_paper" title="Plastic-coated paper">plastic-coated paper</a> milk cartons were introduced commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milk_history_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-milk_history-47">[47]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IndustralizedMilk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3767" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/IndustralizedMilk.jpg/220px-IndustralizedMilk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/IndustralizedMilk.jpg/330px-IndustralizedMilk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/IndustralizedMilk.jpg/440px-IndustralizedMilk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IndustralizedMilk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Industrialized milk.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1863, French chemist and biologist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> invented pasteurization, a method of killing harmful bacteria in beverages and food products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milk_history_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-milk_history-47">[47]</a></sup> He developed this method while on summer vacation in <a href="/wiki/Arbois" title="Arbois">Arbois</a>, to remedy the frequent acidity of the local wines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VR1928_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-VR1928-48">[48]</a></sup> He found out experimentally that it is sufficient to heat a young wine to only about 50–60 °C (122–140 °F) for a brief time to kill the microbes, and that the wine could be nevertheless properly <a href="/wiki/Aging_of_wine" title="Aging of wine">aged</a> without sacrificing the final quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VR1928_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-VR1928-48">[48]</a></sup> In honor of Pasteur, the process became known as "pasteurization". Pasteurization was originally used as a way of preventing wine and beer from souring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Commercial pasteurizing equipment was produced in Germany in the 1880s, and producers adopted the process in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> by 1885.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HwangHuang2009_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-HwangHuang2009-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG/220px-Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG/330px-Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG/440px-Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farm_in_sogn_og_fjordane.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dairy_farm" title="Dairy farm">dairy farm</a> in Norway</div></div></div>
<p>The females of all mammal species can, by definition, produce milk, but cow's milk dominates commercial production. In 2011, FAO estimates 85% of all milk worldwide was produced from cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs12_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-gs12-52">[52]</a></sup> Human milk is not produced or distributed industrially or commercially; however, <a href="/wiki/Human_milk_bank" title="Human milk bank">human milk banks</a> collect donated human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breastmilk" title="Breastmilk">breastmilk</a> and redistribute it to infants who may benefit from human milk for various reasons (premature neonates, babies with allergies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inborn_error_of_metabolism" title="Inborn error of metabolism">metabolic diseases</a>, etc.) but who cannot breastfeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Western world, cow's milk is produced on an industrial scale and is, by far, the most commonly consumed form of milk. Commercial dairy farming using <a href="/wiki/Automatic_milking" title="Automatic milking">automated milking</a> equipment produces the vast majority of milk in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dairy_cattle" title="Dairy cattle">Dairy cattle</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holstein_cattle" title="Holstein cattle">Holstein</a>, have been bred selectively for increased milk production. About 90% of the dairy cows in the United States and 85% in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">Great Britain</a> are Holsteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup> Other dairy cows in the United States include <a href="/wiki/Ayrshire_cattle" title="Ayrshire cattle">Ayrshire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_Swiss" title="Brown Swiss">Brown Swiss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guernsey_cattle" title="Guernsey cattle">Guernsey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey_cattle" title="Jersey cattle">Jersey</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milking_Shorthorn" title="Milking Shorthorn">Milking Shorthorn</a> (Dairy Shorthorn).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animal-based_sources">Other animal-based sources</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Other significant sources of milk</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:100px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goat_family.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Goat_family.jpg/150px-Goat_family.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Goat_family.jpg/225px-Goat_family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Goat_family.jpg/300px-Goat_family.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Goats (2% of world's milk)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:136px;max-width:136px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:100px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_buffaloes_Sri_Lanka_grazing.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Water_buffaloes_Sri_Lanka_grazing.jpg/134px-Water_buffaloes_Sri_Lanka_grazing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Water_buffaloes_Sri_Lanka_grazing.jpg/201px-Water_buffaloes_Sri_Lanka_grazing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Water_buffaloes_Sri_Lanka_grazing.jpg/268px-Water_buffaloes_Sri_Lanka_grazing.jpg 2x" width="134"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Buffaloes (11%)</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Aside from cattle, many kinds of livestock provide milk used by humans for dairy products. These animals include <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo#Dairy_products" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat_milk" title="Goat milk">goat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep_milk" title="Sheep milk">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camel_milk" title="Camel milk">camel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ass%27s_milk_(Donkey%27s_milk)" title="Ass's milk (Donkey's milk)">donkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse#Products" title="Horse">horse</a>, reindeer and yak. The first four respectively produced about 11%, 2%, 1.4% and 0.2% of all milk worldwide in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs12_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-gs12-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Russia and Sweden, small <a href="/wiki/Moose_milk" title="Moose milk">moose dairies</a> also exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the US National Bison Association, <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">American bison</a> (also called American buffalo) are not milked commercially;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> however, various sources report cows resulting from cross-breeding bison and domestic cattle are good milk producers, and have been used both during the European settlement of North America<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and during the development of commercial <a href="/wiki/Beefalo" title="Beefalo">Beefalo</a> in the 1970s and 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swine" title="Swine">Swine</a> are almost never milked, even though <a href="/wiki/Pig_milk" title="Pig milk">their milk</a> is similar to cow's milk and perfectly suitable for human consumption. The main reasons for this are that milking a sow's numerous small teats is very cumbersome, and that sows can not store their milk as cows can.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> A few pig farms do sell pig cheese as a novelty item; these cheeses are exceedingly expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_worldwide">Production worldwide</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">Dairy farming</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1034237262">.mw-parser-output .stack{box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .stack>div{margin:1px;overflow:hidden}@media all and (min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-left{float:left;clear:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-right{float:right;clear:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-clear-left{float:left;clear:left;margin-right:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-clear-right{float:right;clear:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-left{float:left;margin-right:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-right{float:right;margin-left:1em}}</style><div class="mw-stack stack-container stack-left"><div>
<table class="wikitable sortable">
<caption>Largest milk producers in the world<br/>in 2018<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>In 2012, the largest producer of milk and milk products was India followed by the United States of America, China, Pakistan and Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> All 28 European Union members together produced 153.8 million tonnes (169.5 million short tons) of milk in 2013, the largest by any <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">politico</a>-<a href="/wiki/Economic_union" title="Economic union">economic</a> union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Increasing affluence in developing countries, as well as increased promotion of milk and milk products, has led to a rise in milk consumption in developing countries in recent years. In turn, the opportunities presented by these growing markets have attracted investments by <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a> dairy firms. Nevertheless, in many countries production remains on a small scale and presents significant opportunities for diversification of income sources by small farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Local milk collection centers, where milk is collected and chilled prior to being transferred to urban dairies, are a good example of where farmers have been able to work on a <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperative</a> basis, particularly in countries such as India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_yields">Production yields</span></h3>
<p>FAO reports<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs12_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-gs12-52">[52]</a></sup> Israel dairy farms are the most productive in the world, with a yield of 12,546 kilograms (27,659 lb) milk per cow per year. This survey over 2001 and 2007 was conducted by ICAR (International Committee for Animal Recording)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> across 17 developed countries. The survey found that the average herd size in these developed countries increased from 74 to 99 cows per herd between 2001 and 2007. A dairy farm had an average of 19 cows per herd in Norway, and 337 in New Zealand. Annual milk production in the same period increased from 7,726 to 8,550 kg (17,033 to 18,850 lb) per cow in these developed countries. The lowest average production was in New Zealand at 3,974 kg (8,761 lb) per cow. The milk yield per cow depended on production systems, nutrition of the cows, and only to a minor extent different genetic potential of the animals. What the cow ate made the most impact on the production obtained. New Zealand cows with the lowest yield per year grazed all year, in contrast to Israel with the highest yield where the cows ate in barns with an energy-rich mixed diet.
</p><p>The milk yield per cow in the United States was 9,954 kg (21,945 lb) per year in 2010. In contrast, the milk yields per cow in India and China<span class="nowrap"> </span>– the second and third largest producers<span class="nowrap"> </span>– were respectively 1,154 kg (2,544 lb) and 2,282 kg (5,031 lb) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milk_Prices.webp"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="362" data-file-width="1497" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milk_Prices.webp/300px-Milk_Prices.webp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milk_Prices.webp/450px-Milk_Prices.webp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milk_Prices.webp/600px-Milk_Prices.webp.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milk_Prices.webp" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Milk price per gallon of whole milk</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="1588" decoding="async" height="77" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp/300px-Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp/450px-Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp/600px-Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corn_vs_Ethanol_production.webp" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Corn vs Ethanol production in the United States <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FFD932; color:black;"> </span> Total corn production (<a href="/wiki/Bushel" title="Bushel">bushels</a>) (left)</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#B51700; color:white;"> </span> Corn used for Ethanol fuel (bushels) (left)</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-line mw-no-invert" style="display: inline-block; vertical-align: middle; width: 1.67em; height: 0; border-style: none; border-top: 2px dotted black;border-top:#313131 solid 3px;"> </span> Percent of corn used for Ethanol (right)</div></div></div></div>
<p>It was reported in 2007 that with increased worldwide prosperity and the competition of bio-fuel production for feed stocks, both the demand for and the price of milk had substantially increased worldwide. Particularly notable was the rapid increase of consumption of milk in China and the rise of the price of milk in the United States above the government subsidized price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In 2010 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">Department of Agriculture</a> predicted farmers would receive an average of $1.35 per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_gallon" title="US gallon">US gallon</a> ($0.36/l; $1.62/imp gal) of cow's milk, which is down 30 cents per US gallon (7.9 ¢/l; 36 ¢/imp gal) from 2007 and below the <a href="/wiki/Break-even" title="Break-even">break-even</a> point for many cattle farmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition"><span class="anchor" id="Physical_and_chemical_properties"></span>Composition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TriglycerideDairyButter.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="1240" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/TriglycerideDairyButter.png/220px-TriglycerideDairyButter.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/TriglycerideDairyButter.png/330px-TriglycerideDairyButter.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/TriglycerideDairyButter.png/440px-TriglycerideDairyButter.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TriglycerideDairyButter.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Butterfat is a <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triglyceride</a> (fat) formed from fatty acids such as <a href="/wiki/Myristic_acid" title="Myristic acid">myristic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmitic_acid" title="Palmitic acid">palmitic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acids</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Milk is an <a href="/wiki/Emulsion" title="Emulsion">emulsion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloid</a> of <a href="/wiki/Butterfat" title="Butterfat">butterfat</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globules_of_fat" title="Globules of fat">globules</a> within a water-based fluid that contains dissolved <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and protein aggregates with minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Because it is produced as a food source for the young, all of its contents provide benefits for growth. The principal requirements are energy (lipids, lactose, and protein), biosynthesis of non-essential amino acids supplied by proteins (essential amino acids and amino groups), essential fatty acids, vitamins and inorganic elements, and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="pH">pH</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of milk ranges from 6.7 to 6.9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Milk from other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bovines" title="Bovines">bovines</a> and non-bovine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a> varies in composition, but has a similar pH.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lipids">Lipids</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Butterfat" title="Butterfat">Butterfat</a></div>
<p>Initially milk fat is secreted in the form of a fat globule surrounded by a <a href="/wiki/Milk_fat_globule_membrane" title="Milk fat globule membrane">membrane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1995_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1995-77">[77]</a></sup> Each fat globule is composed almost entirely of triacylglycerols and is surrounded by a membrane consisting of complex lipids such as <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>, along with proteins. These act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emulsifier" title="Emulsifier">emulsifiers</a> which keep the individual globules from coalescing and protect the contents of these globules from various <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> in the fluid portion of the milk. Although 97–98% of lipids are triacylglycerols, small amounts of di- and monoacylglycerols, free cholesterol and cholesterol esters, free fatty acids, and phospholipids are also present. Unlike protein and carbohydrates, fat composition in milk varies widely due to genetic, lactational, and nutritional factor difference between different species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1995_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1995-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like composition, fat globules vary in size from less than 0.2 to about 15 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometers</a> in diameter between different species. Diameter may also vary between animals within a species and at different times within a milking of a single animal. In unhomogenized cow's milk, the fat globules have an average diameter of two to four micrometers and with homogenization, average around 0.4 micrometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1995_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1995-77">[77]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fat-soluble" title="Fat-soluble">fat-soluble</a> vitamins <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">D</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_E" title="Vitamin E">E</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">K</a> along with essential fatty acids such as linoleic and linolenic acid are found within the milk fat portion of the milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proteins">Proteins</span></h3>
<p>Normal bovine milk contains 30–35 grams of protein per liter of which about 80% is arranged in casein <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micelles" title="Micelles">micelles</a>. Total proteins in milk represent 3.2% of its composition (nutrition table).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Caseins">Caseins</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Casein" title="Casein">Casein</a></div>
<p>The largest structures in the fluid portion of the milk are <a href="/wiki/Casein" title="Casein">"casein micelles"</a>: aggregates of several thousand protein molecules with superficial resemblance to a surfactant <a href="/wiki/Micelle" title="Micelle">micelle</a>, bonded with the help of nanometer-scale particles of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_phosphate" title="Calcium phosphate">calcium phosphate</a>. Each casein micelle is roughly spherical and about a tenth of a micrometer across. There are four different types of casein proteins: αs1-, αs2-, β-, and κ-caseins. Most of the casein proteins are bound into the micelles. There are several competing theories regarding the precise structure of the micelles, but they share one important feature: the outermost layer consists of strands of one type of protein, <a href="/wiki/K-casein" title="K-casein">k-casein</a>, reaching out from the body of the micelle into the surrounding fluid. These kappa-casein molecules all have a negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_charge" title="Electrical charge">electrical charge</a> and therefore repel each other, keeping the micelles separated under normal conditions and in a stable <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloidal</a> <a href="/wiki/Suspension_(chemistry)" title="Suspension (chemistry)">suspension</a> in the water-based surrounding fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chem_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-chem-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Milk contains dozens of other types of proteins beside caseins and including enzymes. These other proteins are more water-soluble than caseins and do not form larger structures. Because the proteins remain suspended in <a href="/wiki/Whey" title="Whey">whey</a> remaining when caseins coagulate into curds, they are collectively known as <i>whey proteins</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lactoglobulin" title="Lactoglobulin">Lactoglobulin</a> is the most common whey protein by a large margin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup> The ratio of caseins to whey proteins varies greatly between species; for example, it is 82:18 in cows and around 32:68 in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applications2017_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Applications2017-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span id="Salts.2C_minerals.2C_and_vitamins"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Salts,_minerals,_and_vitamins">Salts, minerals, and vitamins</span></h3>
<p>Minerals or milk salts, are traditional names for a variety of cations and anions within bovine milk. Calcium, phosphate, magnesium, sodium, potassium, citrate, and chloride are all included and they typically occur at concentrations of 5–40<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Molar_concentration#Units" title="Molar concentration">mM</a>. The milk salts strongly interact with casein, most notably calcium phosphate. It is present in excess and often, much greater excess of solubility of solid calcium phosphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-74">[74]</a></sup> In addition to calcium, milk is a good source of many other vitamins. Vitamins A, B6, B12, C, D, K, E, thiamine, niacin, biotin, riboflavin, folates, and pantothenic acid are all present in milk.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Calcium_phosphate_structure">Calcium phosphate structure</span></h4>
<p>For many years the most widely accepted theory of the structure of a micelle was that it was composed of spherical casein aggregates, called submicelles, that were held together by calcium phosphate linkages. However, there are two recent models of the casein micelle that refute the distinct micellular structures within the micelle.
</p><p>The first theory, attributed to de Kruif and Holt, proposes that nanoclusters of calcium phosphate and the phosphopeptide fraction of beta-casein are the centerpiece to micellar structure. Specifically in this view unstructured proteins organize around the calcium phosphate, giving rise to their structure, and thus no specific structure is formed.
</p><p>Under the second theory, proposed by Horne, the growth of calcium phosphate nanoclusters begins the process of micelle formation, but is limited by binding phosphopeptide loop regions of the caseins. Once bound, protein-protein interactions are formed and polymerization occurs, in which K-casein is used as an end cap to form micelles with trapped calcium phosphate nanoclusters.
</p><p>Some sources indicate that the trapped calcium phosphate is in the form of Ca<sub>9</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>6</sub>;
whereas others say it is similar to the structure of the mineral <a href="/wiki/Brushite" title="Brushite">brushite</a>, CaHPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sugars_and_carbohydrates">Sugars and carbohydrates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="526" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg/220px-Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg/330px-Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg/440px-Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrolysis_of_lactose.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simplified representation of a <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> molecule being broken down into <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> (2) and <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a> (1)</div></div></div>
<p>Milk contains several different <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> including <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>, and other oligosaccharides. The lactose gives milk its sweet taste and contributes approximately 40% of whole cow's milk's calories. Lactose is a disaccharide composite of two <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">simple sugars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>. Bovine milk averages 4.8% anhydrous lactose, which amounts to about 50% of the total solids of skimmed milk. Levels of lactose are dependent upon the type of milk as other carbohydrates can be present at higher concentrations than lactose in milks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellaneous_contents">Miscellaneous contents</span></h3>
<p>Other components found in raw cow's milk are living <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a>, mammary gland cells, various bacteria, vitamin C, and a large number of active enzymes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance">Appearance</span></h3>
<p>Both the fat globules and the smaller casein micelles, which are just large enough to deflect light, contribute to the opaque white color of milk. The fat globules contain some yellow-orange carotene, enough in some breeds (such as Guernsey and Jersey cattle) to impart a golden or "creamy" hue to a glass of milk. The <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">riboflavin</a> in the whey portion of milk has a greenish color, which sometimes can be discerned in skimmed milk or whey products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup> Fat-free skimmed milk has only the casein micelles to scatter light, and they tend to scatter shorter-wavelength blue light more than they do red, giving skimmed milk a bluish tint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chem_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-chem-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Processing">Processing</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milkproducts_v2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="1323" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Milkproducts_v2.svg/220px-Milkproducts_v2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Milkproducts_v2.svg/330px-Milkproducts_v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Milkproducts_v2.svg/440px-Milkproducts_v2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milkproducts_v2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Milk products and productions relationships (click to enlarge)</div></div></div>
<p>In most Western countries, centralized dairy facilities process milk and <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">products obtained from milk</a>, such as cream, butter, and cheese. In the US, these dairies usually are local companies, while in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> facilities may be run by large multi-national corporations such as <a href="/wiki/Fonterra" title="Fonterra">Fonterra</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pasteurization">Pasteurization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization#Milk" title="Pasteurization">Pasteurization § Milk</a></div>
<p>Pasteurization is used to kill harmful <a href="/wiki/Pathogenic_bacteria" title="Pathogenic bacteria">pathogenic bacteria</a> such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M._paratuberculosis" title="M. paratuberculosis">M. paratuberculosis</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._coli_0157:H7" title="E. coli 0157:H7">E. coli 0157:H7</a></i> by heating the milk for a short time and then immediately cooling it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holzinger97_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-holzinger97-82">[82]</a></sup> Types of pasteurized milk include full cream, reduced fat, skim milk, calcium enriched, flavored, and UHT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The standard high temperature short time (HTST) process of 72 °C (162 °F) for 15 seconds completely kills pathogenic bacteria in milk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> rendering it safe to drink for up to three weeks if continually refrigerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Dairies print <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Best_before" title="Best before">best before</a> dates on each container, after which stores remove any unsold milk from their shelves.
</p><p>A side effect of the heating of pasteurization is that some vitamin and mineral content is lost. Soluble calcium and phosphorus decrease by 5%, thiamin and vitamin B12 by 10%, and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> by 20% or greater (even to complete loss).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Because losses are small in comparison to the large amount of the two B-vitamins present, milk continues to provide significant amounts of thiamin and vitamin B12. The loss of vitamin C is not nutritionally significant in a well-balanced diet, as milk is not an important dietary source of vitamin C.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Filtration">Filtration</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Microfiltration" title="Microfiltration">Microfiltration</a> is a process that partially replaces pasteurization and produces milk with fewer microorganisms and longer shelf life without a change in the taste of the milk. In this process, cream is separated from the skimmed milk and is pasteurized in the usual way, but the skimmed milk is forced through ceramic microfilters that trap 99.9% of microorganisms in the milk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> (as compared to 99.999% killing of microorganisms in standard <a href="/wiki/Flash_pasteurization" title="Flash pasteurization">HTST pasteurization</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The skimmed milk then is recombined with the pasteurized cream to reconstitute the original milk composition.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ultrafiltration" title="Ultrafiltration">Ultrafiltration</a> uses finer filters than microfiltration, which allow lactose and water to pass through while retaining fats, calcium and protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson2015_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2015-89">[89]</a></sup> As with microfiltration, the fat may be removed before filtration and added back in afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncse_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncse-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ultrafiltered_milk" title="Ultrafiltered milk">Ultrafiltered milk</a> is used in cheesemaking, since it has reduced volume for a given protein content, and is sold directly to consumers as a higher protein, lower sugar content, and creamier alternative to regular milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Creaming_and_homogenization">Creaming and homogenization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1760" data-file-width="2070" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg/220px-Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg/330px-Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg/440px-Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cow_milking_machine_in_action_DSC04132.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A milking machine in action</div></div></div>
<p>Upon standing for 12 to 24 hours, fresh milk has a tendency to separate into a high-fat cream layer on top of a larger, low-fat milk layer. The cream often is sold as a separate product with its own uses. Today the separation of the cream from the milk usually is accomplished rapidly in <a href="/wiki/Centrifuge" title="Centrifuge">centrifugal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cream_separator" title="Cream separator">cream separators</a>. The fat globules rise to the top of a container of milk because fat is less dense than water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-7"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The smaller the globules, the more other molecular-level forces prevent this from happening. The cream rises in cow's milk much more quickly than a simple model would predict: rather than isolated globules, the fat in the milk tends to form into clusters containing about a million globules, held together by a number of minor whey proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-8"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup> These clusters rise faster than individual globules can. The fat globules in milk from goats, sheep, and water buffalo do not form clusters as readily and are smaller to begin with, resulting in a slower separation of cream from these milks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-9"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Milk often is <a href="/wiki/Homogenization_(chemistry)" title="Homogenization (chemistry)">homogenized</a>, a treatment that prevents a cream layer from separating out of the milk. The milk is pumped at high pressures through very narrow tubes, breaking up the fat globules through <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cavitation" title="Cavitation">cavitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> A greater number of smaller particles possess more total <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> than a smaller number of larger ones, and the original fat globule membranes cannot completely cover them. Casein micelles are attracted to the newly exposed fat surfaces.
</p><p>Nearly one-third of the micelles in the milk end up participating in this new membrane structure. The casein weighs down the globules and interferes with the clustering that accelerated separation. The exposed fat globules are vulnerable to certain enzymes present in milk, which could break down the fats and produce <a href="/wiki/Rancidification" title="Rancidification">rancid</a> flavors. To prevent this, the enzymes are inactivated by pasteurizing the milk immediately before or during homogenization.
</p><p>Homogenized milk tastes blander but feels creamier in the mouth than unhomogenized. It is whiter and more resistant to developing off flavors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-10"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup> Creamline (or cream-top) milk is unhomogenized. It may or may not have been pasteurized. Milk that has undergone high-pressure homogenization, sometimes labeled as "ultra-homogenized", has a longer <a href="/wiki/Shelf_life" title="Shelf life">shelf life</a> than milk that has undergone ordinary homogenization at lower pressures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UHT">UHT</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ultra-high-temperature_processing" title="Ultra-high-temperature processing">Ultra Heat Treatment</a> (UHT) is a type of milk processing where all bacteria are destroyed with high heat to extend its shelf life for up to 6 months, as long as the package is not opened. Milk is firstly homogenized and then is heated to 138 degrees Celsius for 1–3<span class="nowrap"> </span>seconds. The milk is immediately cooled down and packed into a sterile container. As a result of this treatment, all the pathogenic bacteria within the milk are destroyed, unlike when the milk is just pasteurized. The treated milk will keep for up for 6 months if unopened. UHT milk does not need to be refrigerated until the package is opened, which makes it easier to ship and store. However, in this process there is a loss of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitamin_B1" title="Vitamin B1">vitamin B1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>, and there is also a slight change in the taste of the milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition_and_health">Nutrition and health</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fat_content_of_milk" title="Fat content of milk">Fat content of milk</a></div>
<p>The composition of milk differs widely among species. Factors such as the type of protein; the proportion of protein, fat, and sugar; the levels of various vitamins and minerals; and the size of the butterfat globules, and the strength of the curd are among those that may vary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-intro-24">[24]</a></sup> For example:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Breast_milk" title="Breast milk">Human milk</a> contains, on average, 1.1% protein, 4.2% fat, 7.0% lactose (a sugar), and supplies 72 kcal of energy per 100 grams.</li>
<li>Cow's milk contains, on average, 3.4% protein, 3.6% fat, and 4.6% lactose, 0.7% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">minerals</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and supplies 66 kcal of energy per 100 grams. See also <a href="#Nutritional_value">Nutritional value</a> further on in this article and more complete lists at online sources that list values and differences in categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Donkey and horse milk have the lowest fat content, while the milk of <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a> and whales may contain more than 50% fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span id="Cow.27s_milk:_variation_by_breed"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cow's_milk:_variation_by_breed">Cow's milk: variation by breed</span></h3>
<p>These compositions vary by breed, animal, and point in the lactation period.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Milk fat percentages
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Cow breed
</th>
<th>Approximate percentage
</th></tr>
<tr>
