</td>
<td>5.2
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">Zebu</a>
</td>
<td>4.7
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_Swiss" title="Brown Swiss">Brown Swiss</a>
</td>
<td>4.0
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holstein_cattle" title="Holstein cattle">Holstein-Friesian</a>
</td>
<td>3.6
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The protein range for these four breeds is 3.3% to 3.9%, while the lactose range is 4.7% to 4.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_Food_and_Cooking_22-11"><a href="#cite_note-On_Food_and_Cooking-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Milk fat percentages may be manipulated by dairy farmers' stock diet formulation strategies. The infection known as <a href="/wiki/Mastitis" title="Mastitis">mastitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mastitis_in_dairy_cattle" title="Mastitis in dairy cattle">especially in dairy cattle</a>, can cause fat levels to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutritional_value">Nutritional value</span></h3>
<p>Processed cow's milk was formulated to contain differing amounts of fat during the 1950s. One cup (250 mL) of 2%-fat cow's milk contains 285 mg of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, which represents 22% to 29% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_recommended_intake" title="Daily recommended intake">daily recommended intake</a> (DRI) of calcium for an adult. Depending on its age, milk contains 8 grams of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and a number of other nutrients<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2022)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> (either naturally or through <a href="/wiki/Food_fortification" title="Food fortification">fortification</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergy">Allergy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Milk_allergy" title="Milk allergy">Milk allergy</a></div>
<p>One of the most common <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">food allergies</a> in infants is to cow's milk. This is an immunologically mediated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adverse_reaction" title="Adverse reaction">adverse reaction</a>, rarely fatal, to one or more cow's milk proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Milk allergy affects between 2% and 3% of babies and young children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savage2015_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Savage2015-100">[100]</a></sup> To reduce risk, recommendations are that babies should be exclusively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breastfed" title="Breastfed">breastfed</a> for at least four months, preferably six months, before introducing cow's milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The majority of children outgrow milk allergy, but for about 0.4% the condition persists into adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu2010_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liu2010-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lactose_intolerance">Lactose intolerance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lactose_intolerance" title="Lactose intolerance">Lactose intolerance</a></div>
<p>Lactose intolerance is a condition in which people have symptoms due to deficiency or absence of the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Lactase" title="Lactase">lactase</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a>, causing poor absorption of milk lactose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2014Dig_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2014Dig-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deng2015_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deng2015-104">[104]</a></sup> People affected vary in the amount of lactose they can tolerate before symptoms develop,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2014Dig_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2014Dig-103">[103]</a></sup> which may include abdominal pain, <a href="/wiki/Bloating" title="Bloating">bloating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, gas, and <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2014Dig_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2014Dig-103">[103]</a></sup> Severity depends on the amount of milk consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2014Dig_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2014Dig-103">[103]</a></sup> Those affected are usually able to drink at least one cup of milk without developing significant symptoms, with greater amounts tolerated if drunk with a meal or throughout the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2014Dig_103-4"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2014Dig-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuchyBrannon2010_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-SuchyBrannon2010-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_lactation">Evolution of lactation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lactation" title="Lactation">Lactation</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary gland</a> is thought to have derived from <a href="/wiki/Apocrine" title="Apocrine">apocrine</a> skin glands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oftedal_2002_225–252_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oftedal_2002_225–252-106">[106]</a></sup> It has been suggested that the original function of lactation (milk production) was keeping eggs moist. Much of the argument is based on <a href="/wiki/Monotreme" title="Monotreme">monotremes</a> (egg-laying mammals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oftedal_2002_225–252_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oftedal_2002_225–252-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The original adaptive significance of milk secretions may have been nutrition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> or immunological protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> This secretion gradually became more copious and accrued nutritional complexity over evolutionary time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oftedal_2002_225–252_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oftedal_2002_225–252-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tritylodontidae" title="Tritylodontidae">Tritylodontid</a> <a href="/wiki/Cynodont" title="Cynodont">cynodonts</a> seem to have displayed lactation, based on their dental replacement patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bovine_growth_hormone_supplementation">Bovine growth hormone supplementation</span></h2>
<p>Since November 1993, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recombinant_bovine_somatotropin" title="Recombinant bovine somatotropin">recombinant bovine somatotropin</a> (rbST), also called rBGH, has been sold to dairy farmers with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FDA" title="FDA">FDA</a> approval. Cows produce bovine growth hormone naturally, but some producers administer an additional recombinant version of BGH which is produced through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_engineered" title="Genetically engineered">genetically engineered</a> <a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a> to increase milk production. Bovine growth hormone also stimulates liver production of <a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor_1" title="Insulin-like growth factor 1">insulin-like growth factor 1</a> (IGF1). The US <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> have reported that both of these compounds are safe for human consumption at the amounts present.
</p><p>Milk from cows given rBST may be sold in the United States, and the FDA stated that no significant difference has been shown between milk derived from rBST-treated and that from non-rBST-treated cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Milk that advertises that it comes from cows not treated with rBST, is required to state this finding on its label.
</p><p>Cows receiving rBGH supplements may more frequently contract an udder infection known as <a href="/wiki/Mastitis" title="Mastitis">mastitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Problems with mastitis have led to Canada, Australia, New Zealand, and Japan banning milk from rBST treated cows. Mastitis, among other diseases, may be responsible for the fact that levels of <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a> in milk vary naturally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>rBGH is also banned in the European Union, for reasons of animal welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties_and_brands">Varieties and brands</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">Dairy product</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg/170px-Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg/255px-Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg/340px-Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dairy_Crest_Semi_Skimmed_Milk_Bottle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Glass milk bottle used for home delivery service in the UK</div></div></div>
<p>Milk products are sold in a number of varieties based on types/degrees of:
</p>
<ul><li>additives (e.g. vitamins, flavorings)</li>
<li>age (e.g. cheddar, old cheddar)</li>
<li>coagulation (e.g. cottage cheese)</li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Hay_milk"></span>farming method (e.g. organic, grass-fed, <a href="/wiki/Haymilk" title="Haymilk">haymilk</a>)</li>
<li>fat content (e.g. half and half, 3% fat milk, 2% milk, 1% milk, skim milk)</li>
<li>fermentation (e.g. buttermilk)</li>
<li>flavoring (e.g. chocolate and strawberry)</li>
<li>homogenization (e.g. cream top)</li>
<li>packaging (e.g. bottle, carton, bag)</li>
<li>pasteurization (e.g. raw milk, pasteurized milk)</li>
<li>reduction or elimination of lactose</li>
<li>species (e.g. cow, goat, sheep)</li>
<li>sweetening (e.g., chocolate and strawberry milk)</li>
<li>water content (e.g. dry milk powder, condensed milk, ultrafiltered milk)</li></ul>
<p>Milk preserved by the <a href="/wiki/Ultra-high-temperature_processing" title="Ultra-high-temperature processing">UHT</a> process does not need to be refrigerated before opening and has a much longer shelf life (six months) than milk in ordinary packaging. It is typically sold unrefrigerated in the UK, US, Europe, Latin America, and Australia.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reduction_or_elimination_of_lactose">Reduction or elimination of lactose</span></h3>
<p>Lactose-free milk can be produced by passing milk over lactase enzyme bound to an inert carrier. Once the molecule is cleaved, there are no lactose ill effects. Forms are available with reduced amounts of lactose (typically 30% of normal), and alternatively with nearly 0%. The only noticeable difference from regular milk is a slightly sweeter taste due to the cleavage of lactose into glucose and galactose. Lactose-reduced milk can also be produced via <a href="/wiki/Ultrafiltered_milk" title="Ultrafiltered milk">ultra filtration</a>, which removes smaller molecules such as lactose and water while leaving calcium and proteins behind. Milk produced via these methods has a lower sugar content than regular milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson2015_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2015-89">[89]</a></sup> To aid digestion in those with <a href="/wiki/Lactose_intolerance" title="Lactose intolerance">lactose intolerance</a>, another alternative is dairy foods, milk and <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, with added bacterial cultures such as <i><a href="/wiki/Lactobacillus_acidophilus" title="Lactobacillus acidophilus">Lactobacillus acidophilus</a></i> ("acidophilus milk") and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bifidobacteria" title="Bifidobacteria">bifidobacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Another milk with <i><a href="/wiki/Lactococcus_lactis" title="Lactococcus lactis">Lactococcus lactis</a></i> bacteria cultures ("<a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">cultured buttermilk</a>") often is used in cooking to replace the traditional use of naturally <a href="/wiki/Soured_milk" title="Soured milk">soured milk</a>, which has become rare due to the ubiquity of pasteurization, which also kills the naturally occurring Lactococcus bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additives_and_flavoring">Additives and flavoring</span></h3>
<p>Commercially sold milk commonly has <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> added to it to make up for lack of exposure to <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UVB</a> radiation. Reduced-fat milks often have added <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitamin_A_palmitate" title="Vitamin A palmitate">vitamin A palmitate</a> to compensate for the loss of the vitamin during fat removal; in the United States this results in reduced fat milks having a higher vitamin A content than whole milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Milk often has <a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">flavoring</a> added to it for better taste or as a means of improving sales. <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_milk" title="Chocolate milk">Chocolate milk</a> has been sold for many years and has been followed more recently by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strawberry_milk" title="Strawberry milk">strawberry milk</a> and others. Some nutritionists have criticized <a href="/wiki/Flavored_milk" title="Flavored milk">flavored milk</a> for adding sugar, usually in the form of <a href="/wiki/High-fructose_corn_syrup" title="High-fructose corn syrup">high-fructose corn syrup</a>, to the diets of children who are already commonly obese in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glass_milk_bottles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Glass_milk_bottles.jpg/170px-Glass_milk_bottles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Glass_milk_bottles.jpg/255px-Glass_milk_bottles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Glass_milk_bottles.jpg/340px-Glass_milk_bottles.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glass_milk_bottles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Returning reusable glass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_bottle" title="Milk bottle">milk bottles</a>, used for home delivery service in the UK</div></div></div>
<p>Due to the short shelf life of normal milk, it used to be delivered to households daily in many countries; however, improved refrigeration at home, changing food shopping patterns because of supermarkets, and the higher cost of home delivery mean that daily deliveries by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milkman" title="Milkman">milkman</a> are no longer available in most countries.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia_and_New_Zealand">Australia and New Zealand</span></h4>
<p>In Australia and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, prior to <a href="/wiki/Metrication" title="Metrication">metrication</a>, milk was generally distributed in 1 pint (568<span class="nowrap"> </span>mL) glass bottles. In Australia and Ireland there was a government funded "free milk for school children" program, and milk was distributed at morning recess in 1/3 pint bottles. With the conversion to metric measures, the milk industry was concerned that the replacement of the pint bottles with 500<span class="nowrap"> </span>mL bottles would result in a 13.6% drop in milk consumption; hence, all pint bottles were recalled and replaced by 600<span class="nowrap"> </span>mL bottles. With time, due to the steadily increasing cost of collecting, transporting, storing and cleaning glass bottles, they were replaced by cardboard cartons. A number of designs were used, including a tetrahedron which could be close-packed without waste space, and could not be knocked over accidentally (slogan: "No more crying over spilt milk"). However, the industry eventually settled on a design similar to that used in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwwa_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwwa-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Milk is now available in a variety of sizes in paperboard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_carton" title="Milk carton">milk cartons</a> (250 mL, 375 mL, 600 mL, 1 liter and 1.5 liters) and <a href="/wiki/Plastic_bottle" title="Plastic bottle">plastic bottles</a> (1, 2 and 3 liters). A significant addition to the marketplace has been "long-life" milk (<a href="/wiki/Ultra-high-temperature_processing" title="Ultra-high-temperature processing">UHT</a>), generally available in 1 and 2 liter rectangular cardboard cartons. In urban and suburban areas where there is sufficient demand, home delivery is still available, though in suburban areas this is often 3 times per week rather than daily. Another significant and popular addition to the marketplace has been flavored milks; for example, as mentioned above, <a href="/wiki/Farmers_Union_Iced_Coffee" title="Farmers Union Iced Coffee">Farmers Union Iced Coffee</a> outsells <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_,_Gagar_,used_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_%2C_Gagar_%2Cused_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg/220px-Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_%2C_Gagar_%2Cused_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_%2C_Gagar_%2Cused_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg/330px-Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_%2C_Gagar_%2Cused_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_%2C_Gagar_%2Cused_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg/440px-Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_%2C_Gagar_%2Cused_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milk_vender_with_typical_traditional_brass_containers_,_Gagar_,used_in_Majha_Region_of_Punjab_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vendors in <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a>,India transporting milk in <a href="/wiki/Gagar" title="Gagar">gagar</a>, 2019</div></div></div>
<p>In rural <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, milk is home delivered, daily, by local milkmen carrying bulk quantities in a metal container, usually on a bicycle. In other parts of metropolitan <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, milk is usually bought or delivered in plastic bags or cartons via shops or supermarkets.
</p><p>The current milk chain flow in India is from milk producer to milk collection agent. Then it is transported to a milk chilling center and bulk transported to the processing plant, then to the sales agent and finally to the consumer.
</p><p>A 2011 survey by the Food Safety and Standards Authority of India found that nearly 70% of samples had not conformed to the standards set for milk. The study found that due to lack of hygiene and sanitation in milk handling and packaging, detergents (used during cleaning operations) were not washed properly and found their way into the milk. About 8% of samples in the survey were found to have detergents, which are hazardous to health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, milk is supplied in jugs. Milk has been a staple food, especially among the pastoral tribes in this country.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span></h4>
<p>Since the late 1990s, milk-buying patterns have changed drastically in the UK. The classic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milkman" title="Milkman">milkman</a>, who travels his local <a href="/wiki/Milk_round" title="Milk round">milk round</a> (route) using a <a href="/wiki/Milk_float" title="Milk float">milk float</a> (often battery powered) during the early hours and delivers milk in 1-<a href="/wiki/Pint" title="Pint">pint</a> glass bottles with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum_foil" title="Aluminum foil">aluminum foil</a> tops directly to households, has almost disappeared. Two of the main reasons for the decline of UK home deliveries by milkmen are household refrigerators (which lessen the need for daily milk deliveries) and private car usage (which has increased supermarket shopping). Another factor is that it is cheaper to purchase milk from a supermarket than from home delivery. In 1996, more than 2.5 billion liters of milk were still being delivered by milkmen, but by 2006 only 637 million liters (13% of milk consumed) was delivered by some 9,500 milkmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> By 2010, the estimated number of milkmen had dropped to 6,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Assuming that delivery per milkman is the same as it was in 2006, this means milkmen deliveries now only account for 6–7% of all milk consumed by UK households (6.7 billion liters in 2008/2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Almost 95% of all milk in the UK is thus sold in shops today, most of it in plastic bottles of various sizes, but some also in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_carton" title="Milk carton">milk cartons</a>. Milk is hardly ever sold in glass bottles in UK shops.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4>
<p>In the United States, glass milk bottles have been replaced mostly with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_carton" title="Milk carton">milk cartons</a> and plastic jugs. Gallons of milk are almost always sold in jugs, while half gallons and quarts may be found in both paper cartons and plastic jugs, and smaller sizes are almost always in cartons.
</p><p>The "half pint" (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>237 mL, <span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">5</span>⁄<span class="den">12</span></span> imp pt) milk carton is the traditional unit as a component of school lunches, though some companies have replaced that unit size with a plastic bottle, which is also available at retail in 6- and 12-pack size.
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Four_litre_bagged_milk,_Quebec.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1836" data-file-width="3661" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Four_litre_bagged_milk%2C_Quebec.jpg/220px-Four_litre_bagged_milk%2C_Quebec.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Four_litre_bagged_milk%2C_Quebec.jpg/330px-Four_litre_bagged_milk%2C_Quebec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Four_litre_bagged_milk%2C_Quebec.jpg/440px-Four_litre_bagged_milk%2C_Quebec.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Four_litre_bagged_milk,_Quebec.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four liter bagged milk in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, Canada</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg/200px-Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg/300px-Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg/400px-Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mejeriprodukter_i_Sk%C3%A5nsk_butik.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The milk section in a Swedish grocery store</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="666" data-file-width="448" decoding="async" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg/220px-Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg/330px-Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg/440px-Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glass_School_Milk_Bottle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A primary school child in England drinking milk out of a glass bottle with a straw</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milk-bottle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="792" data-file-width="528" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Milk-bottle.jpg/170px-Milk-bottle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Milk-bottle.jpg/255px-Milk-bottle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Milk-bottle.jpg/340px-Milk-bottle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milk-bottle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A glass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_bottle" title="Milk bottle">bottle</a> of non-homogenized, organic, local milk from the US state of California. American milk bottles are generally rectangular in shape.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1790" data-file-width="1013" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG/170px-Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG/255px-Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG/340px-Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kirkland_Milk_Jug.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Square_milk_jug" title="Square milk jug">rectangular milk jug</a> design used by <a href="/wiki/Costco" title="Costco">Costco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam%27s_Club" title="Sam's Club">Sam's Club</a> stores in the United States which allows for stacking and display of filled containers rather than being shipped to the store in <a href="/wiki/Milk_crate" title="Milk crate">milk crates</a> and manual loading into a freezer display rack</div></div></div>
<p>Glass milk bottles are now rare. Most people purchase milk in bags, plastic bottles, or plastic-coated paper cartons. <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a> (UV) light from <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lighting</a> can alter the flavor of milk, so many companies that once distributed milk in <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">transparent</a> or highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translucent" title="Translucent">translucent</a> containers are now using thicker materials that block the UV light.
Milk comes in a variety of <a href="/wiki/Carton" title="Carton">containers</a> with local variants:
</p>
<dl><dt>Argentina</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 1-liter bags and cardboard boxes. The bag is then placed in a plastic jug and the corner cut off before the milk is poured.</dd>
<dt>Australia and New Zealand</dt>
<dd>Distributed in a variety of sizes, most commonly in <a href="/wiki/Aseptic_processing" title="Aseptic processing">aseptic</a> cartons for up to 1.5 liters, and plastic screw-top bottles beyond that with the following volumes; 1.1 L, 2 L, and 3 L. 1-liter <a href="/wiki/Milk_bag" title="Milk bag">milk bags</a> are starting to appear in supermarkets, but have not yet proved popular. Most UHT-milk is packed in 1 or 2 liter paper containers with a sealed plastic spout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwwa_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-wwwa-125">[125]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>Brazil</dt>
<dd>Used to be sold in cooled 1-liter bags, just like in South Africa. Today the most common form is 1-liter aseptic cartons containing UHT skimmed, semi-skimmed or whole milk, although the plastic bags are still in use for pasteurized milk. Higher grades of pasteurized milk can be found in cartons or plastic bottles. Sizes other than 1-liter are rare.</dd></dl>
<dl><dt>Canada</dt>
<dd>1.33 liter plastic bags (sold as 4 liters in 3 bags) are widely available in some areas (especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maritimes" title="Maritimes">Maritimes</a>, Ontario and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>), although the 4 liter plastic jug has supplanted them in western Canada. Other common packaging sizes are 2 liter, 1 liter, 500 mL, and 250 mL cartons, as well as 4 liter, 1 liter, 250 mL aseptic cartons and 500 mL plastic jugs.</dd>
<dt>Chile</dt>
<dd>Distributed most commonly in <a href="/wiki/Aseptic_processing" title="Aseptic processing">aseptic</a> cartons for up to 1 liter, but smaller, snack-sized cartons are also popular. The most common flavors, besides the natural presentation, are chocolate, strawberry and vanilla.</dd>
<dt>China</dt>
<dd>Sweetened milk is a drink popular with students of all ages and is often sold in small plastic bags complete with straw. Adults not wishing to drink at a banquet often drink milk served from cartons or <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong-style_milk_tea" title="Hong Kong-style milk tea">milk tea</a>.</dd>
<dt>Colombia</dt>
<dd>Sells milk in 1-liter plastic bags.</dd>
<dt>Croatia, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Serbia, Montenegro</dt>
<dd>UHT milk (<i>trajno mlijeko/trajno mleko/</i>трајно млеко) is sold in 500 mL and 1 L (sometimes also 200 mL) aseptic cartons. Non-UHT pasteurized milk (<i>svježe mlijeko/sveže mleko/</i>свеже млеко) is most commonly sold in 1 L and 1.5 L PET bottles, though in Serbia one can still find milk in plastic bags.</dd>
<dt>Estonia</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 1 L bags or 0.33 L, 0.5 L, 1 L or 1.5 L cartons.</dd>
<dt>Parts of Europe</dt>
<dd>Sizes of 500 mL, 1 liter (the most common), 1.5 liters, 2 liters and 3 liters are commonplace.</dd>
<dt>Finland</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 1 L or 1.5 L cartons, in some places also in 2 dl and 5 dl cartons.</dd>
<dt>Germany</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 1-liter cartons. Sale in 1-liter plastic bags (common in the 1980s) is now rare.</dd>
<dt>Hong Kong</dt>
<dd>Milk is sold in glass bottles (220 mL), cartons (236 mL and 1 L), plastic jugs (2 liters) and aseptic cartons (250 mL).</dd>
<dt>India</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 500 mL plastic bags and in bottles in some parts like in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>. It is still customary to serve the milk boiled, despite pasteurization. Milk is often buffalo milk. Flavored milk is sold in most convenience stores in waxed cardboard containers. Convenience stores also sell many varieties of milk (such as flavored and ultra-pasteurized) in various sizes, usually in aseptic cartons.</dd>
<dt>Indonesia</dt>
<dd>Usually sold in 1-liter cartons, but smaller, snack-sized cartons are available.</dd>
<dt>Italy</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 1-liter cartons or bottles and less commonly in 0.5 or 0.25-liter cartons. Whole milk, semi-skimmed milk, skimmed, lactose-free, and flavored (usually in small packages) milk is available. Milk is sold fresh or UHT. Goat's milk is also available in small amounts. UHT semi-skimmed milk is the most sold, but cafés use almost exclusively fresh whole milk.</dd>
<dt>Japan</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 1-liter waxed paperboard cartons. In most city centers there is also <a href="/wiki/Delivery_(commerce)" title="Delivery (commerce)">home delivery</a> of milk in glass jugs. As seen in China, sweetened and flavored milk drinks are commonly seen in <a href="/wiki/Vending_machine" title="Vending machine">vending machines</a>.</dd>
<dt>Kenya</dt>
<dd>Milk in Kenya is mostly sold in plastic-coated aseptic paper cartons supplied in 300 mL, 500 mL or 1 liter volumes. In rural areas, milk is stored in plastic bottles or gourds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tracing_the_origin_of_Mursik_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tracing_the_origin_of_Mursik-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The standard unit of measuring milk quantity in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> is a liter.</dd>
<dt>Pakistan</dt>
<dd>Milk is supplied in 500 mL plastic bags and carried in jugs from rural to cities for selling</dd>
<dt>Philippines</dt>
<dd>Milk is supplied in 1000 mL plastic bottles and delivered from factories to cities for selling.</dd>
<dt>Poland</dt>
<dd>UHT milk is mostly sold in aseptic cartons (500 mL, 1 L, 2 L), and non-UHT in 1 L plastic bags or plastic bottles. Milk, UHT is commonly boiled, despite being pasteurized.</dd>
<dt>South Africa</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 1-liter bags. The bag is then placed in a plastic jug and the corner cut off before the milk is poured.</dd>
<dt>South Korea</dt>
<dd>Sold in cartons (180 mL, 200 mL, 500 mL 900 mL, 1 L, 1.8 L, 2.3 L), plastic jugs (1 L and 1.8 L), aseptic cartons (180 mL and 200 mL) and plastic bags (1 L).</dd>
<dt>Sweden</dt></dl>
<dl><dd>Commonly sold in 0.3 L, 1 L or 1.5 L cartons and sometimes as plastic or glass milk bottles.</dd>
<dt>Turkey</dt>
<dd>Commonly sold in 500 mL or 1L cartons or special plastic bottles. UHT milk is more popular. Milkmen also serve in smaller towns and villages.</dd>
<dt>United Kingdom</dt>
<dd>Most stores stock <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">imperial</a> sizes: 1 pint (568 mL), 2 pints (1.136 L), 4 pints (2.273 L), 6 pints (3.408 L) or a combination including both metric and imperial sizes. Glass milk bottles delivered to the doorstep by the milkman are typically pint-sized and are returned empty by the householder for repeated <a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">reuse</a>. Milk is sold at supermarkets in either aseptic cartons or HDPE bottles. Supermarkets have also now begun to introduce milk in bags, to be poured from a proprietary jug and nozzle.</dd></dl>
<dl><dt>United States</dt></dl>
<dl><dd>Commonly sold in <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">gallon</a> (3.78 L), half-gallon (1.89 L) and <a href="/wiki/Quart" title="Quart">quart</a> (0.94 L) containers of natural-colored HDPE resin, or, for sizes less than one gallon, cartons of waxed paperboard. Bottles made of opaque <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">PET</a> are also becoming commonplace for smaller, particularly metric, sizes such as one liter. The US single-serving size is usually the half-pint (about 240 mL). Less frequently, dairies deliver milk directly to consumers, from coolers filled with glass bottles which are typically half-gallon sized and returned for reuse. Some convenience store chains in the United States (such as <a href="/wiki/Kwik_Trip" title="Kwik Trip">Kwik Trip</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midwest" title="Midwest">Midwest</a>) sell milk in half-gallon bags, while another <a href="/wiki/Square_milk_jug" title="Square milk jug">rectangular cube gallon container design</a> used for easy stacking in shipping and displaying is used by <a href="/wiki/Warehouse_club" title="Warehouse club">warehouse clubs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Costco" title="Costco">Costco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam%27s_Club" title="Sam's Club">Sam's Club</a>, along with some <a href="/wiki/Walmart" title="Walmart">Walmart</a> stores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>Uruguay</dt>
<dd>Pasteurized milk is commonly sold in 1-liter bags and ultra-pasteurized milk is sold in cardboard boxes called <a href="/wiki/Tetra_Brik" title="Tetra Brik">Tetra Briks</a>. Non-pasteurized milk is forbidden. Until the 1960s no treatment was applied; milk was sold in bottles. As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Milk&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, plastic jugs used for pouring the bags, or "sachets", are in common use.</dd></dl>
<p>Practically everywhere, <a href="/wiki/Condensed_milk" title="Condensed milk">condensed milk</a> and evaporated milk are distributed in metal cans, 250 and 125 mL paper containers and 100 and 200 mL squeeze tubes, and <a href="/wiki/Powdered_milk" title="Powdered milk">powdered milk</a> (skim and whole) is distributed in boxes or bags.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spoilage_and_fermented_milk_products">Spoilage and fermented milk products</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fermented_milk_products" title="Fermented milk products">Fermented milk products</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yakult_drink.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3652" data-file-width="2556" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yakult_drink.jpg/170px-Yakult_drink.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yakult_drink.jpg/255px-Yakult_drink.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yakult_drink.jpg/340px-Yakult_drink.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yakult_drink.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yakult" title="Yakult">Yakult</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Probiotic" title="Probiotic">probiotic</a> milk-like product made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermenting</a> a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Skimmed_milk" title="Skimmed milk">skimmed milk</a> with a special strain of the bacterium <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lactobacillus_casei" title="Lactobacillus casei">Lactobacillus casei</a> Shirota</i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg/170px-Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg/255px-Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg/340px-Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kipsigis_Gourd.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gourd used by <a href="/wiki/Kalenjin_people" title="Kalenjin people">Kalenjins</a> to prepare a local version of fermented milk called <i><a href="/wiki/Mursik" title="Mursik">mursik</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tracing_the_origin_of_Mursik_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tracing_the_origin_of_Mursik-131">[131]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Raw_milk" title="Raw milk">raw milk</a> is left standing for a while, it turns "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sour" title="Sour">sour</a>". This is the result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid_bacteria" title="Lactic acid bacteria">lactic acid bacteria</a> ferment the <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> in the milk into <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>. Prolonged fermentation may render the milk unpleasant to consume. This fermentation process is exploited by the introduction of bacterial cultures (e.g. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lactobacilli" title="Lactobacilli">Lactobacilli</a> sp., <a href="/wiki/Streptococcus" title="Streptococcus">Streptococcus</a> sp., <a href="/wiki/Leuconostoc" title="Leuconostoc">Leuconostoc</a> sp.</i>, etc.) to produce a variety of <a href="/wiki/Fermented_milk_products" title="Fermented milk products">fermented milk products</a>. The reduced pH from lactic acid accumulation denatures proteins and causes the milk to undergo a variety of different transformations in appearance and texture, ranging from an aggregate to smooth consistency. Some of these products include <a href="/wiki/Sour_cream" title="Sour cream">sour cream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, cheese, <a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">buttermilk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viili" title="Viili">viili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kefir" title="Kefir">kefir</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kumis" title="Kumis">kumis</a>. <i>See <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">Dairy product</a></i> for more information.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">Pasteurization</a> of cow's milk initially destroys any potential pathogens and increases the shelf life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> but eventually results in spoilage that makes it unsuitable for consumption. This causes it to assume an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unpleasant_odor" title="Unpleasant odor">unpleasant odor</a>, and the milk is deemed non-consumable due to unpleasant taste and an increased risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">food poisoning</a>. In raw milk, the presence of lactic acid-producing bacteria, under suitable conditions, ferments the lactose present to lactic acid. The increasing <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidity</a> in turn prevents the growth of other organisms, or slows their growth significantly. During pasteurization, however, these lactic acid bacteria are mostly destroyed.
</p><p>In order to prevent spoilage, milk can be kept <a href="/wiki/Refrigerator" title="Refrigerator">refrigerated</a> and stored between 1 and 4 °C (34 and 39 °F) in <a href="/wiki/Bulk_tank" title="Bulk tank">bulk tanks</a>. Most milk is pasteurized by heating briefly and then refrigerated to allow transport from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factory_farming" title="Factory farming">factory farms</a> to local markets. The spoilage of milk can be forestalled by using ultra-high temperature (<a href="/wiki/Ultra-high-temperature_processing" title="Ultra-high-temperature processing">UHT</a>) treatment. Milk so treated can be stored unrefrigerated for several months until opened but has a characteristic "cooked" taste. <a href="/wiki/Condensed_milk" title="Condensed milk">Condensed milk</a>, made by removing most of the water, can be stored in cans for many years, unrefrigerated, as can <a href="/wiki/Evaporated_milk" title="Evaporated milk">evaporated milk</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Powdered_milk">Powdered milk</span></h3>
<p>The most durable form of milk is <a href="/wiki/Powdered_milk" title="Powdered milk">powdered milk</a>, which is produced from milk by removing almost all water. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moisture_content" title="Moisture content">moisture content</a> is usually less than 5% in both drum- and spray-dried powdered milk.
</p><p>Freezing of milk can cause fat globule aggregation upon thawing, resulting in milky layers and butterfat lumps. These can be dispersed again by warming and stirring the milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hui2006_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-hui2006-136">[136]</a></sup> It can change the taste by destruction of milk-fat globule membranes, releasing oxidized flavors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hui2006_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-hui2006-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_other_food_products">Use in other food products</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steamed_milk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="1140" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Steamed_milk.jpg/200px-Steamed_milk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Steamed_milk.jpg/300px-Steamed_milk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Steamed_milk.jpg/400px-Steamed_milk.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steamed_milk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Steamed_milk&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Steamed milk (page does not exist)">Steamed milk</a> is used in a variety of <a href="/wiki/Espresso" title="Espresso">espresso</a>-based coffee beverages.</div></div></div>
<p>Milk is used to make <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, cheese, <a href="/wiki/Ice_milk" title="Ice milk">ice milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pudding" title="Pudding">pudding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_chocolate" title="Hot chocolate">hot chocolate</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_toast" title="French toast">french toast</a>, among many other products. Milk is often added to dry <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_cereal" title="Breakfast cereal">breakfast cereal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porridge" title="Porridge">porridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Granola" title="Granola">granola</a>. Milk is mixed with ice cream and flavored syrups in a blender to make <a href="/wiki/Milkshake" title="Milkshake">milkshakes</a>. Milk is often served in coffee and tea. Frothy steamed milk is used to prepare <a href="/wiki/Espresso" title="Espresso">espresso</a>-based drinks such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cafe_latte" title="Cafe latte">cafe latte</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_language_and_culture">In language and culture</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3872" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg/170px-From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg/255px-From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg/340px-From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:From_Abhisheka_To_Panchamrutha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hindu <a href="/wiki/Abhisheka" title="Abhisheka">Abhisheka</a> ritual in <a href="/wiki/Agara,_Bangalore_Rural" title="Agara, Bangalore Rural">Agara</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangalore_Rural_District" title="Bangalore Rural District">Bangalore Rural District</a>, Karnataka</div></div></div>
<p>The importance of milk in human culture is attested to by the numerous expressions embedded in our languages, for example, "the milk of human kindness", the expression "there's no use crying over spilt milk" (which means don't "be unhappy about what cannot be undone"), "don't milk the ram" (this means "to do or attempt something futile") and "Why buy a cow when you can get milk for free?" (which means "why pay for something that you can get for free otherwise").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> was formed after the <a href="/wiki/Trickster" title="Trickster">trickster</a> god <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> suckled the infant <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> at the breast of <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>, the queen of the gods, while she was asleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-139">[139]</a></sup> When Hera awoke, she tore Heracles away from her breast and splattered her breast milk across the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-139">[139]</a></sup> In another version of the story, <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, the patron goddess of heroes, tricked Hera into suckling Heracles voluntarily,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-139">[139]</a></sup> but he bit her nipple so hard that she flung him away, spraying milk everywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_138-3"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_139-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many African and Asian countries, butter is traditionally made from fermented milk rather than cream. It can take several hours of churning to produce workable butter grains from fermented milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Holy books have also mentioned milk. The Bible contains references to the "<a href="/wiki/Land_of_Israel" title="Land of Israel">Land of Milk and Honey</a>" as a metaphor for the bounty of the Promised Land. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>, there is a request to wonder on milk as follows: "And surely in the livestock there is a lesson for you, We give you to drink of that which is in their bellies from the midst of digested food and blood, pure milk palatable for the drinkers" (16-The Honeybee, 66). The <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> fast is traditionally broken with a glass of milk and dates.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abhisheka" title="Abhisheka">Abhisheka</a> is conducted by <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain</a> priests, by pouring libations on the <a href="/wiki/Murti" title="Murti">idol of a deity</a> being worshipped, amidst the chanting of <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a>. Usually offerings such as milk, <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghee" title="Ghee">ghee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> may be poured among other offerings depending on the type of abhishekam being performed.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Milksop" title="Milksop">milksop</a> is an "effeminate spiritless man," an expression which is attested to in the late 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline.com_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline.com-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Milk_toast" title="Milk toast">Milk toast</a> is a dish consisting of milk and toast. Its soft blandness served as inspiration for the name of the timid and ineffectual <a href="/wiki/Comic_strip" title="Comic strip">comic strip</a> character <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Milquetoast" title="Caspar Milquetoast">Caspar Milquetoast</a>, drawn by <a href="/wiki/H._T._Webster" title="H. T. Webster">H. T. Webster</a> from 1924 to 1952.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Thus, the term "milquetoast" entered the language as the label for a timid, shrinking, apologetic person. Milk toast also appeared in Disney's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Follow_Me_Boys" title="Follow Me Boys">Follow Me Boys</a></i> as an undesirable breakfast for the aging main character Lem Siddons.
</p><p>To "milk" someone, in the vernacular of many English-speaking countries, is to take advantage of the person, by analogy to the way a farmer "milks" a cow and takes its milk. The word "milk" has had many slang meanings over time. In the 19th century, milk was used to describe a cheap and very poisonous alcoholic drink made from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylated_spirits" title="Methylated spirits">methylated spirits</a> (methanol) mixed with water. The word was also used to mean defraud, to be idle, to intercept telegrams addressed to someone else, and a weakling or "milksop." In the mid-1930s, the word was used in Australia to refer to siphoning gas from a car.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-culinary_uses">Non-culinary uses</span></h2>
<p>Besides serving as a beverage or source of food, milk has been described as used by farmers and gardeners as an organic <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicide</a> and fertilizer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> however, its effectiveness is debated. Diluted milk solutions have been demonstrated to provide an effective method of preventing powdery mildew on grape vines, while showing it is unlikely to harm the plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Milk_paint" title="Milk paint">Milk paint</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nontoxic" title="Nontoxic">nontoxic</a> water-based <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paint</a>. It can be made from milk and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">lime</a>, generally with <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigments</a> added for color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beecher_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beecher-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transvaal_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Transvaal-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baird_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baird-150">[150]</a></sup> In other recipes, <a href="/wiki/Borax" title="Borax">borax</a> is mixed with milk's casein protein in order to activate the <a href="/wiki/Casein" title="Casein">casein</a> and as a preservative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PS42_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-PS42-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wailes_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wailes-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_(2413988302).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_%282413988302%29.jpg/220px-Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_%282413988302%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_%282413988302%29.jpg/330px-Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_%282413988302%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_%282413988302%29.jpg/440px-Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_%282413988302%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milk_bath_spa_in_DVN_(2413988302).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A milk and rose-petal bath at a spa in Thailand</div></div></div>
<p>Milk has been used for centuries as a hair and skin treatment. 
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arterbery_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arterbery-153">[153]</a></sup> Hairstylist Richard Marin states that some women rinse their hair with milk to add a shiny appearance to their hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arterbery_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arterbery-153">[153]</a></sup> Cosmetic chemist Ginger King states that milk can "help exfoliate and remove debris [from skin] and make hair softer. Hairstylist Danny Jelaca states that milk's keratin proteins may "add weight to the hair".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arterbery_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-Arterbery-153">[153]</a></sup> Some commercial hair products contain milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arterbery_153-3"><a href="#cite_note-Arterbery-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Milk_bath" title="Milk bath">milk bath</a> is a bath taken in milk rather than just water. Often additives such as oatmeal, <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, and scents such as <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">rose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asteraceae" title="Asteraceae">daisies</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_oils" title="Essential oils">essential oils</a> are mixed in. Milk baths use <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_hydroxy_acid" title="Alpha hydroxy acid">alpha hydroxy acid</a>, to dissolve the proteins which hold together dead skin cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_milk_consumption">Interspecies milk consumption</span></h2>
<p>The consumption of milk between species is not unique to humans. Although the occurrence of this utilization is not vastly documented, interspecies consumption of milk has been observed among the <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_oxpecker" title="Red-billed oxpecker">red-billed oxpecker</a> —a bird that can perch on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Udders" title="Udders">udders</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Impala" title="Impala">impala</a> and suck its milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seagulls" title="Seagulls">Seagulls</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheathbills" title="Sheathbills">Sheathbills</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skuas" title="Skuas">Skuas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_gull" title="Western gull">Western Gulls</a> and African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feral_cats" title="Feral cats">feral cats</a> have been reported to directly pilfer milk from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elephant_seals" title="Elephant seals">elephant seals</a>’ teats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Jewish.2FKosher_milk"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish/Kosher_milk">Jewish/Kosher milk</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chalav_Yisrael" title="Chalav Yisrael">Chalav Yisrael</a> is the term of <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Jewish religious law</a> regulating consumption of milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
